[25/10/2025, 15:28:51] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: Anyone here have experience with federated training and federated fine‑tuning?
I'm currently exploring ways to fine-tune models on user‑health data (including PII and health profiles), but we want to keep that data encrypted / private and not lift raw data from the device or the user account. Anyone who has done something similar in health or other regulated industries?

Thanks in advance!
[25/10/2025, 17:13:35] ~ Chris Beanland: ‎~ Chris Beanland was added

[25/10/2025, 18:21:30] ~ Philip Taylor: ‎~ Philip Taylor was added
[25/10/2025, 18:49:48] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: Happy to share more detail.

We’re already fine-tuning models for multi-turn health conversations to ensure our agents gives accurate answers and keeps the right tone (empathetic, friendly, adheres to NICE guidelines, etc). We use a custom-built human-in-the-loop platform for review and feedback, which has worked really well for our base models so far.

Now, as we roll out to real users, we want to improve those models further using actual, opted-in user behaviour and interaction data. BUT, all user data is encrypted and stored privately on-device/by account, so we never see raw PII or health data in the cloud.

That’s why we’re exploring federated fine-tuning as a way to keep learning from real-world usage, without ever centralising or decrypting sensitive data. I realise this is still relatively novel territory, but you never know if anyone’s tried something similar in health/AI 👀

[26/10/2025, 10:15:39] Harshitha Shetty: ‎Harshitha Shetty was added
[26/10/2025, 13:11:46] Lilli Buettner: ‎Lilli Buettner was added
[27/10/2025, 10:09:06] Alistair Groves: ‎Alistair Groves was added
[27/10/2025, 10:26:56] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: Thanks for the follow-up, Erik!

In a nutshell, the idea is to never have a central pool of user data, not even anonymised.

In the federated fine-tuning approach, the raw data (PII/health info) never leaves the user’s device or account. Instead, the model is fine-tuned locally (on-device or in the user’s private cloud), and then only the resulting model updates (essentially learned parameters...not the underlying data itself) are shared and aggregated to improve the centralised/global model. But as I mentioned, it's relatively novel, so there aren't many good resources available.

Re: your other questions:

- Our users are primarily end users (not clinicians), and privacy and compliance are absolutely core.

- We are exploring synthetic data for model validation, but for real-world relevance, we want the models to learn from actual, opted-in usage (While still keeping everyone’s data private).

[28/10/2025, 07:22:51] Erik Schwartz: Brilliant Mikkel, thanks for painting a clearer picture for us.   And now I understand better why you are asking for others who might be attempting such a bold plan. 

So for clarification purposes, are you running fully distributed inference?   Or are your models hosted centrally, and you are just passing personalized context (encrypted patient and health data, PII / GDPR compliant) in from distributed devices. 

I would imagine the former as the compute requirements are still quite rigorous for full distributed inference and patients will have a wide variety of devices. 

Stop me if I’m getting to close to your IP
[28/10/2025, 08:33:48] ~ Martijn Versteeg: Ahw man, now I’m curious what Mikkel said 🤣 too bad there’s no history on whatsapp from begore you join. ‎<This message was edited>
[28/10/2025, 10:09:25] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: All inference is currently done centrally in the cloud. We’ve experimented a bit with running models locally on mobile devices, but quickly ran into a couple of big hurdles:

- App size balloons fast. Even with relatively simple models (low number of training parameters), you easily end up with a 2GB app.

- The quality and speed of on-device inference just isn’t there yet compared to cloud-hosted models.

So for now, the models are cloud-hosted.

But it’s no secret that long-term, we’d love to get to a place where inference and data storage can both be fully local. True privacy, end-to-end. But that tech is still a few years out.
[29/10/2025, 06:32:00] Erik Schwartz: We are discussing @⁨~Mikkel Nielsen⁩ ‘s federated fine tuning for an app serving the health markets 

I was asking some clarifying questions to help understand the approach and user base. 

The motivations are to protect patient health data and PII.

As he says above, truly federated fine tuning - at the device level - just isn’t feasible as app size gets massive. 

So right now it’s being done at the private cloud level, which could also create some really challenging scale problems if every user needs their own private cloud for model inference. 

One suggestion to look at, which is an approach that’s being taken in highly regulated and security concerned environments is to look at on demand inference.  

Effectively the way this works is that at the time of inference, when the model is invoked, a docker container is spun up for the duration of the session. And after the session it is destroyed. 

Fine tuning would have to be done offline, in batch mode, so there would be some latency but this might be acceptable if users engage with app as daily check in, for example. 

Docker, or kubernetes, images could be spun up for fine tuning runs, model parameters could be persisted, and then the containers are destroyed. 

Still requires a massive amount of storage, as you are effectively creating a different container for every user, but it would free up GPU to help manage scale. 

Super challenging problem. You are definitely at the bleeding edge with this problem.

‎[12/11/2025, 10:17:27] ~ Mike Hogan: Has anyone had any real horror stories with using AI? I have this recent example in our business:

We recently did a full product and infrastructure review using AI. It went through all our repos and transcripts from meetings we had with the architects to “understand” each product. The results were presented straight to leadership without anyone validating them with the individual product leads. I got pulled up about the supposed “state” of my product.

Turns out the AI’s method was completely off. It rated one of our best-performing platforms as almost failing. When we looked closer and used the right analysis, it showed the product was actually top-tier. (Screenshot after the "rerun")

The takeaway for me is that AI can be really useful, but only if people actually understand what it’s doing. You can’t take the output at face value. It still needs human sense-checking before it drives decisions. ‎image omitted
[12/11/2025, 10:19:14] ~ Mike Hogan: Granted, it was the individuals using it that were at fault, not the model itself

[12/11/2025, 10:30:36] ~ Ryan Musselwhite: I’m slowly introducing AI into our product, the key is to solve actual pain points rather than having AI for the sake of having AI (tick in the box). Each use case we introduce, we’re doing some pretty heavy internal validation across teams before pulling in some alpha customers. It’s going well so far but certainly playing it safe.

Also rolling out AI across product and engineering but in a similar vein. Looking to have some modest gains of efficiencies, around 10%. A lot of this usage is around reducing the manual effort on lower complexity items, using AI as an assistant more akin to an intern.
[12/11/2025, 10:32:48] ~ Ryan Musselwhite: Some other information you may find useful, our PE investors have seen evaluations of roughly 20% higher for products that have AI in them. So if you’re planning an exit of your business and have equity, it’s worth bearing that in mind.

[14/11/2025, 09:47:13] Erik Schwartz: yeah, I love this pattern - it’s almost identical to what I’m taking execs and product leaders through in my day long workshop —

1. PRD and ideation in GPT.

3. ⁠Finish the backend with Claude Code and feature refinement as I still find Lovable is quite chatty with tokens.

[19/11/2025, 13:38:58] ~ Sascha Brossmann: 1. Claude Code does NOT implement RAG but you can use https://github.com/yichuan-w/LEANN to add retrieval (CC can use it like any other tool)
2. ⁠My general approach: let CC follow the same practices and process that you would use if you did it yourself and cared a lot for quality.
3. ⁠LLMs amplify what’s salient (due to the reinforcement learning) and struggle with catching subtle information. The larger the document the worse => keep your files small and focused, break down larger files. It can help to process documents block by block (example: tagging topics and concepts in interviews) and create intermediate files for each step in the process. I always do sanity checks for each step.
[19/11/2025, 13:44:03] Erik Schwartz: This is much closer to what’s going on behind the scenes. 

Two separate scenarios to think about:
1. Upload a document though chat 
2. ⁠find a document through web search or RAG

They both effectively take on the same principle however which is to reduce content into a multidimensional matrix (ie, vectorized) to map to the internal representation of the data. 

In the former (1) the LLM does this internally, and the latter (2) its done externally in a search engine or a vector database.
[19/11/2025, 13:55:26] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Yep. Caveat: when using local file-based agents like Claude Code, the agent does _not_ store the vectorised data of files it once has scanned for later retrieval. Instead they search for content with regular file-based tools such as _grep_, guided by the information stored in e.g. CLAUDE.md
[19/11/2025, 13:55:59] ~ Ana Woodrow: really useful to know!
[19/11/2025, 14:07:03] ~ Sascha Brossmann: An other important concept to understand is that the chat feature is an illusion. Technically the LLM works with just _one_ growing document that contains everything in one blob: the system prompt, the user prompt, all supplied data etc. It’s still just a very powerful autocomplete with lossy compression. That also implies that especially when working with larger documents the performance rapidly degrades as the context window fills up. Degradation already happens way before reaching the claimed context window capacity. => keep it small, start from new instead of iterating.

[19/11/2025, 19:20:56] Alastair Preacher: Hey! So basically my entire hard drive is now in Obsidian - all docs, downloaded Notion files, everything. All .mds backed up to github (private)

Main workflows:

* Daily planning: I have a Claude command that creates my to-do list in an obsidian template in a specific folder, it pulls in my calendar, grabs yesterday's uncompleted tasks, and lets me add new ones with dates
* Reorganisation: Claude helped me restructure the whole vault (been meaning to do that forever!)
* Content editing: My N8n flow saves article summaries as markdown files linked to Notion. I can open them in Obsidian and live-edit while asking Claude to make changes - basically pair programming for writing
* File management: Claude has functions to move files between folders, schedule content, etc.
* Session continuity: Claude creates a plan doc for every session and updates it as we go, so if I close down I can pick up where we left off
* Cross-referencing: This is the killer feature - when writing newsletters/articles, I just ask Claude "where have I talked about this before?" or "is this contradictory to anything else I've written?" As the vault grows, Claude can surface related content and help me reference my own work

It's genuinely transformative.

[24/11/2025, 19:02:12] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Humans also hallucinate _a lot_ and most people seem to assume that we _do_ have a world concept. Error correction in humans is by and large an evolutionary process, mostly a social one (see also Thomas Kuhn’s seminal work on paradigm changes). Unfortunately, we also did not evolve for truth (as in “true to objective reality”) but for fitness.
[24/11/2025, 19:29:24] ~ Ashwin: Do you think humans' hallucinations are the same (or) comparable to those of LLMs'? I'm not asking about the magnitude but the definition/dimension itself.
[24/11/2025, 19:33:37] ~ Ashwin: I see them as different. In the context of LLMs, it's divergence that progresses through many layers because of the next X predictors' architecture. In humans, there could be different causes and reasons. One might just be how we tend to represent things in an abstract way leading to, let's call them, micro-hallucinations / filling the gaps. There are other reasons too. I'm not an expert in any of these areas. Just curious.
[24/11/2025, 19:36:55] ~ Ashwin: There is no perception in LLMs but there is in humans.
[24/11/2025, 19:46:49] ~ Martha Beith: Also, human intelligence is often less pervasive before it’s proven wrong if it is hallucinating. I don’t speak to several million people before I get told I’m incorrect.
[24/11/2025, 19:46:58] ~ Sascha Brossmann: What precisely do you mean with “perception”? Most of what we claim about human intelligence and our minds is nothing but conjecture.
[24/11/2025, 19:48:09] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Donald Trump and a lot of other people don’t have any issue with that.
[24/11/2025, 19:49:02] ~ Sascha Brossmann: And that’s people in power, often elected by millions of others, mind you.
[24/11/2025, 19:50:10] ~ Ashwin: I'll wait for you to answer my question so that I can better understand where you are coming from. And then, I'll respond.
[24/11/2025, 19:50:29] ~ Sascha Brossmann: => Fitness over objective truth.
[24/11/2025, 20:40:59] ~ Sascha Brossmann: How much time do you have? 🙃 

There’s a plethora of questions to discuss in that context such es e.g. What’s a hallucination? What makes it different from, say, creative writing? What’s the difficulty with retrieval when using lossy compression? 

I’ll try to shortcut: humans and LLMs exhibit some uncomfortably similar characteristics in _some_ aspects such as unreliable retrieval (due to lossy compression). Our brains struggle with all kinds of deterministic procedures, not onyl with exact retrieval. Try e.g. to multiply two 4-digit numbers in your head and you get outcompeted by primitive electronics by orders of magnitude. Ask 10 people on the street how many “R” there are in “strawberry ice cream” and give them 5 sec. to answer. Check the research on the reliability (or lack thereof) of eye witnesses. I could keep going on. 

Does that make human brains and LLMs equivalent? Probably not. We don’t actually know, though: Human intelligence is still a black box. We know a few things about the micro level, very little in respect to structure, then again a few things that we could classify as surface phenomena. We don’t know anything about how what we experience as our mind actually emerges from neuronal activity. Nothing. That knowledge gap in turn mandates that we consider all strong statements about how human brains and AI models differ as pure speculation. Besides there is the challenge with sufficiently precise definitions (i.e. observable, falsifiable, differentiating, …) for all the terms we are colloquially using for that purpose, such as “perception”, “intention”, “reasoning”, “thinking”, “consciousness”, …

In short: I think it’s most productive to cut the self-worth serving illusions and just describe what we’re observing in case of both humans and machines while starting from _the same_ premises (rather than diametrically opposed ones). The underlying mechanisms are not relevant IMHO. The phenomena are, i. e. the behaviour of the system as a whole. ‎<This message was edited>
[24/11/2025, 20:54:50] ~ Caroline Clark: A psychological definition of hallucination is perceiving something that seems real but has no external physical source. That’s different from imagination, in that hallucinations are involuntary whilst imagination (e.g. creative writing) is voluntary, i.e. there is choice in the perception process.
[24/11/2025, 20:56:01] ~ Sascha Brossmann: How do we know that there is choice? 🙂
[24/11/2025, 20:56:43] ~ Ashwin: Thanks. I understand better now. I think it's too strong to state that the differences between brains and AI are pure speculation. We don't understand consciousness but we do know a lot about how they are different. I also tend to think the underlying mechanisms do matter and as we evolve our understanding, it's in humans' best interests to focus on these mechanisms considering safety.
[24/11/2025, 20:57:25] ~ Caroline Clark: Human memory is not only reductive but it’s also reconstructive - and in that way differs from computer memory. When we ‘remember’ we are reconstructing the memory from fragments stored in different areas of the brain (e.g. visual, auditory, emotional, autobiographical, etc) so it’s never the same again. Every time you remember the memory changes slightly.
[24/11/2025, 20:58:19] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Exactly. And that’s conceptually also what LLMs do when they “retrieve” something.
[24/11/2025, 20:59:14] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Humans solved deterministic retrieval and a few other challenges (e.g. calculation) with external systems of record. ‎<This message was edited>
[24/11/2025, 21:01:23] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Culture-historically extremely interesting. See e.g. Walter J. Ong’s classic “Orality and Literacy”
[24/11/2025, 21:04:30] ~ Sascha Brossmann: We can simulate deterministic procedures with our (most likely) probabilistic brains but we need external storage for that.
[24/11/2025, 21:08:52] ~ Caroline Clark: I want to pick up on the statements made around mental arithmetic and counting letters. There are some aspects of cognitive functioning that have been developed over millions of years of evolution - the human eye is one such example. It’s a highly specialised organ that best works in a pair to enable perception across shape, colour and depth. Comparatively speaking, maths and written language are very very new capabilities - mere thousands of years old. Moradev’s paradox says that things we find hard, computers find easy, and vice versa. Which is how we end up with the dog versus doughnut problem in computer vision.
[24/11/2025, 21:16:51] ~ Sascha Brossmann: The eye is also an excellent example how evolution can develop the same concept in a large variety of ways. Which in turn feeds my argument against essentialism 🙂 Equivalent behaviour does not require equivalent implementation.
[24/11/2025, 21:20:37] ~ Sascha Brossmann: BTW, just wondering if that deep nerd dive still is interesting to others in this channel. 😬
[24/11/2025, 21:27:27] ~ Caroline Clark: If I understand your argument correctly it is that humans and LLMs sometimes show similar behaviour, so the underlying mechanisms may not matter, and that we do not understand human cognition well enough to say more.

If that is the case then I disagree, as someone who has studied psychology and neuroscience. There is a lot we do not yet know, but there is also a great deal that we do. Mechanism matters in cognition. Birds and planes both fly, but the structures and physics behind that flight are fundamentally different. Behaviour alone is not enough to draw conclusions about the systems underneath.

I really recommend ‘Being You’ by Professor Anil Seth for more on this topic.
[24/11/2025, 22:04:17] James Engelbert: Rich Mironov has written a good article on this: https://www.mironov.com/ai-boom/
[24/11/2025, 22:04:18] James Engelbert: Gotta share this here too 
[24/11/2025, 22:15:21] Jason Knight: good old Rich!
[24/11/2025, 22:15:31] Jason Knight: I want to be him when I grow up
[25/11/2025, 00:10:39] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Seems like I haven’t been clear enough (it’s late in the day…) My points are:
1) different mechanisms unlike ours can produce intelligent behaviour (see e.g. octopuses) 
2) (corollary) a mechanism that’s different from ours does not disqualify its system’s intelligence and …
2.a) You cannot judge the system behaviour just by the mechanics (Searle’s classical “Chinese Room” argument has long been disproven, yet it keeps reappearing dressed up as Stochastic Parrots as if ca. 40 years of discourse in the philosophy of mind would not exist and nobody knew anything about the emergence of complex behaviours)
3) we _do_ know a few things but most of the terms thrown around in the public debate with the claim of qualifying unique attributes of human intelligence have nonetheless little empirical grounding, if any, and no clear definition. Last but not least because despite our knowledge we have nonetheless massive leaps of faith between low-level concepts and higher-order concepts, paired with a magical belief in neuro imaging that’s primarily helpful for funding departments and publishing papers with interesting conjectures but still does not provide the missing links (unless I missed some real breakthrough research).
4) Most arguments comparing human intelligence and machine intelligence are starting from opposing premises with the human premises unproven. That’s intellectually dishonest. We have to start with the same premises and build the argument from there. 

I sense a lot of fear here (as in: broader society, not this community) and according attempts at denial. Not that machines could become as intelligent as us but rather that our own minds might actually turn out not _that_ special. 

That all said: there are still more than enough arguments that can demonstrate that LLMs likely won’t get us much further to AGI: sensomotorics/embodied cognition, the systems in the human brain that still have no equivalent in AI, the fundamentally social aspects of human intelligence, our evolutionary drivers, the related feedback cycles, etc.

Does that help clarifying?

PS: haven’t read Seth yet, but e.g. Donald Hoffman who as far as I can tell makes a similar case (pop-sci recommendation: “The Case Against Reality”, but there are also enough papers)

[25/11/2025, 16:38:29] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: We treat quality like a flywheel: fine-tune > evaluate > learn > fine-tune again. The models get better each cycle because our training data and evals get better.

Our validation loop, in a nutshell:

1. We fine-tune the model using our curated, multi-turn training data (real examples, rubric-approved, persona-correct)

2. Run structured evals against fixed scenarios, red-team prompts, edge cases, and “expected answer” comparisons

3. Analyse where the model falls short (tone drift, missing empathy, inaccurate explanations, behaviour outside guardrails etc)

4. Add more examples and/or tighten instructions to fix the gaps

5. Fine-tune again

6. Repeat

[01/12/2025, 21:39:49] ~ Ryan Musselwhite: The main two use cases which worked at my company so far were:
1. Product to verify an idea/concept where it wasn’t worth a full blown discovery from day one. These often were just front-end prototypes without any real functionality complexity
2. ⁠Engineering using AI to write small scripts/apps to solve a specific problem, where the investment of their time without AI wouldn’t justify the returns. These have been very tactical and solved some issues for us, where we didn’t have the resources to work on it end to end. The engineers did still review the code/make changes to ensure it met our standards

We are now doing a wider rollout of AI use within engineering, mostly as AI assistants, with a later view to use a more agent approach. The tricky part with the agent approach is how many guard rails we need to put up due to the scale/complexity of our SaaS product.

For fully end to end vibe coded apps in tools like Loveable/Bolt, the issue I think is tech stack/hand off to engineering. I have also seen these apps generally fail once they grow in complexity so I think a lot fear a re-write would be needed once it hits a certain point.

I have created a few vibe coded apps for side businesses, to reduce my spend on tooling for very specific use-cases like uptime monitoring and a help guide repository site. These have worked really well but the use-case was very simplistic…..
[01/12/2025, 21:45:51] ~ Ryan Musselwhite: After speaking to my 30+ developers, as well as many friends who are tech leads across different industries this is what I am hearing
•⁠  ⁠who owns it long-term
Engineering should, as any apps in significant use should comply to defined standards

•⁠  ⁠how much engineers trust/accept AI-generated code
Very few do, I think there is growing trust of AI assisted code like using Cursor as part of their workflow, but not so much for Vibe coded apps where they have far less control of the changes it makes

•⁠  ⁠security/compliance/quality bars
This is a big fear, the fact the big players like Loveable partnered up with a security consultancy firm shows it has a gap today

•⁠  ⁠whether eng prefers to rebuild vs adopt
Tech stack dependant. Engineers can be set on using specific tech they have experience with, very few senior developers use things like Supabase and these all in one solutions.
[01/12/2025, 22:04:14] ~ Sascha Brossmann: With hi-fi prototyping getting cheaper and faster, the hard part _still_ sits further upstream. I’d actually rather double down on discovery, option vetting and org operating system design because more velocity downstream only amplifies what happens earlier on. Besides, building stuff was never the value bottleneck. The actual bottleneck in most cases is an unhealthy mixture of missing capability and unfit organisational structure. Mostly _social_ problems, that is. Companies cannot tool their way out of that.
[02/12/2025, 12:33:39] Erik Schwartz: yes, it’s changing quickly - many engineers are uncertain because they believe they can do a better job, and they probably can. There are lots of places that Ai fits.  progressive engineering teams are figuring it out, writing proper code practices into their Claude.MD or agent.md  — here is a great document about how serious engineering orgs should manage their environments - https://www.lesswrong.com/posts/vpNG99GhbBoLov9og/claude-4-5-opus-soul-document 

But I think @⁨~Sascha Brossmann⁩ is absolutely nailing - the hard part is discovery - the hard part is PMF - the hard part is determining willingness to pay - this can all be done _before_ a proper hand off needs to be done.

[07/01/2026, 06:22:00] ~ Özgün Gülsal: Just catching up on discussions around language learning and AI. I wanted to share a few slightly controversial opinions.

I’ve tried a looot of apps, methods, and courses for vocab, speaking, writing, grammar, TOEFL, etc. over the past 15 years to learn English. I don’t think I have any talent for learning languages at all. I still remember working for days to memorise English books to pass an exam in high school. A bit of a traumatic experience on my side. 😬

I’d recommend thinking about this in 3 separate points.

First, be clear about why you want to learn the language, and be as specific as possible. Everything starts there, and the methods you choose change accordingly.

Second, identify which skill you want to focus on, such as speaking, writing, or vocabulary. Each skill often needs a different method.

Third, understand your learning style, whether you’re a visual learner, a listener, or whatever works best for you.

Based on these 3 points, you’re good to go and try something new.

When it comes to Duolingo, of course there’s science behind it, and many people, including my husband, learnt the language through Duolingo, current streak 756 days, but also practise it with me. So, it’s quite useful if you don’t hate their teaching system and if the language is fairly new to you.

The reason I deleted Duolingo and never went back, even to learn another language, is their aggressive notification strategy. I was having a really tough time, and one of the messages they sent was something like, “you seem to have no interest in learning this language anymore”. I answered out loud and deleted the app immediately after a 90ish day streak.

I tried a few AI English speaking apps last year. None of them was better than ChatGPT.

Eager to hear others’ experiences during the call. Thanks for setting this up!
[07/01/2026, 08:28:45] Jason Knight: Duolingo is not useless, but I find their gamification approach actively works against language learning, but I'm sure it's great for their numbers. You've got people fighting it out over league spots and desperately trying to get as many XP as possible as quickly as possible. Everything is optimised to keep you on the platform doing milquetoast phrases that you'll never use, either to juice the advertising numbers or sell premium subscriptions

[07/01/2026, 09:18:02] Natalia  🚀 Jaszczuk: I gotta stick with Sascha on this - you may have personal preference, but there's never been a significant effect shown to do with any learning style adaptation. So yes it is a learning science myth / neuromyth that just doesn't want to die!

Having spent quite a while building language learning products, I am sadly also in the camp of Duolingo = bs when it comes to learning. There's some other consumer tools that always had a better learning science basis, usually they weren't free for a long time, for good reason. 

The space is going to be very challenged now in terms of how AI will impact it - however, as with most cases like this, many businesses will go bust but new AI solutions will NOT necessarily have greater impact, despite their potential efficacy. Mostly due to one factor no tech yet mastered, vs a human teacher - motivation. If you are one of the few with strong enough intrinsic motivation to learn, you'll learn even from a book. Many of us are motivated extrinsically, especially as adult learners (fear of making mistakes, not wanting to embarass yourself in front of your teacher or peers if learning in a group) which is why human-led teaching will continue to provide greater results at scale. ‎<This message was edited>

[13/01/2026, 09:30:32] Jason Knight: this is the outline: 

Context Engineering: Move beyond single "one-shot" prompts to building persistent, context-aware AI workspaces that serve as a "shared brain" with your entire product knowledge embedded.
Agentic Workflows: Shift from reactive chatting to "operating" with proactive, semi-autonomous AI systems that can plan, search, execute, and self-correct, automating complex tasks like competitive intelligence and data synthesis.
Vibe Coding: Accelerate prototyping by building functional "Proof-of-Life" prototypes using creative, non-technical descriptions ("vibe") to validate product ideas instantly, focusing on speed of learning rather than shipping production code.
Responsible Adoption: Ensure AI-powered workflows are scalable and trustworthy by establishing clear ownership, implementing guardrails, treating AI outputs as decision inputs, and grounding responses using RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) and Evals (evaluations).

[14/01/2026, 15:19:04] ~ Laurence bahrami: I think risk is one lever. But from what I have seen I also think seniority/maturity of the team members and the stage of the product team/business plays a big part in documentation. ‎<This message was edited>
[14/01/2026, 15:39:01] ~ Laurence bahrami: I can definitely relate to the risk driver.

We’re a small, senior team (4 people), so we’re deliberately doing just enough documentation. We plan and document sprints together, tickets are often a single sentence, and we rely heavily on constant conversation and a fast feedback loop.

When I was at National Grid, it was the opposite. Their core work is large-scale infrastructure with significant exposure to risk (public safety, regulation, public opinion), so the same rigour was applied to software. That translated into heavy upfront and ongoing documentation, even for innovative products.

Net effect: something that might take an hour in my current setup could easily take a month+ there 😂

Would definitely be keen to unpack this more.
[14/01/2026, 15:57:20] Ruan Odendaal: AI changes the maths entirely bc the build effort has dropped 10x, so spending months perfecting specs is expensive procrastination IMHO 🙃

[14/01/2026, 16:07:11] Harry Parkes: Show don’t tell?

From my research, the best field experiments we have show c.20–60% productivity gains, mostly on coding tasks and mostly for more junior devs - not an order-of-magnitude reduction in building enterprise platforms end-to-end.

[26/01/2026, 19:21:53] ~ Robin: Has anyone built 0->1 a chatbot/LLM where context and experience are key? I would love someone to walk me through what customer research you did first to understand user needs when it comes to such a new technology, then how you formed and broke your hypothesis into discreet things to test and validate. About to get started in the coming weeks on my first AI initiative and I'm struggling to understand how to approach this when the output is a moving target and there are a lot of assumptions to our idea. TIA!
[26/01/2026, 22:08:01] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: One thing I learned very quickly is that if you treat “AI” as the thing you’re validating, you get stuck fast.

We’re going through this right now with an AI companion in women’s hormonal health (built around longitudinal conversations) where trust, tone and continuity are basically the whole product.

A few things that helped when the output felt like a moving target:

1) Early discovery was entirely human (we didn’t research “AI use cases”):
- Where are people uncertain, anxious, or stuck?
- ⁠What do they hesitate to ask professionals, friends, or Google?
- ⁠What does “helpful” actually mean in that moment?
Conversational AI only showed up later as the right shape of solution.

2) Instead of testing “will this give good answers?”, we tested behaviour:
- If people feel safe thinking out loud, do they engage more deeply?
- ⁠If the system remembers patterns (not just transcripts), does trust increase?
- ⁠Do people come back without being nudged?
Our hypotheses were behavioural and observable, even while outputs were fuzzy.

3) Finetuning came after discovery. We only moved there once the same failures kept repeating in real multi-turn use:
- Tone drifting in sensitive moments
- ⁠Advice being technically right but emotionally wrong
- ⁠The system not knowing when _not_ to answer

4) Initial human-in-the-loop was about taste rather than control: “Is this the right thing to say now?”
That fed back into prompts, policies, and eventually the finetuning data itself.


TL;DR: Treat it as designing a relationship over time instead of shipping intelligence. If you can’t describe the value in one sentence without mentioning AI, you’re probably too far down the stack.

Hope it’s useful. This stuff is messy, but in a good way 🙂

[27/01/2026, 12:42:42] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Thanks for sharing, this is excellent advice! I’d like to double down on every product person’s favourite nemesis: how do we know that we’re successful? :)

Assuming that “AI” primarily means “LLMs” here: a _probabilistic_ system is harder to evaluate than a _deterministic_ one. Specifically: even small variations in the context provided to the model can yield a much larger range of different outcomes. Model performance/quality itself can also vary (e.g. depending on data center load and your tier, but also between versions of a model – updates can break things that were working). 

Consequently staple product methods start breaking, e.g. multivariate testing becomes unreliable due to variability and needs both larger samples and a probabilistic approach. E.g. you might A/B test output _distribution_, test for acceptable output ranges, etc. 

Consistency turns from a system feature we get for free into a design parameter with a budget and trade-offs. 

=> IMHO it’s smart to invest way more into qual research than usual, both generative and evaluative (It’s already smart even without AI, but with much more so ;)) Especially given that quality/defect attribution is more ambiguous (UI & affordances vs. system behaviour). 

Backend observability (hallucination rate, consistency scoring, drift-detection, …) is another should-have capability. 

Generally the scope of evaluation increases considerably.

Nice recent starting reference: https://arxiv.org/html/2601.16740v1

[02/02/2026, 09:47:08] ~ Prasana: At an LBS event, Chief innovation officer of a magic circle law firm talked about the trade offs. They then moved to Harvey but lost on the cost factor as they have to subscribe to specific pricing tier that’s not benefiting them. Organisations are trying different approaches and making decisions based on trade offs specific to them.

[02/02/2026, 12:09:06] ~ Siddarth Shukla: Run a READ-ONLY security audit of this OpenClaw install. Do not change files. Do not install anything. Redact secrets (no tokens/keys/passwords).
Report:
- What user you run as + any sudo/admin access (incl. passwordless sudo).
- Gateway bind IP/port + whether auth is enabled + whether it’s reachable beyond localhost.
- Who can command you (Telegram/Slack allowlists, group permissions).
- Where credentials are referenced (file paths + variable names only; no values).
- Sensitive folders this user could access (e.g., ~/.ssh, ~/.aws, ~/.config) without reading contents.
- Logs location + whether logs might capture secrets + pending updates.

Rate each risk Low/Med/High/Critical. 
Don’t apply fixes unless I say: APPLY item#.

[05/02/2026, 17:00:43] Matt Stone: I’m testing ChatGPT as a sales channel.

The idea: one click loads a big prompt into someone’s LLM of choice (basically a long header request). 
Because people share real context with their AI (& that is stored in memory), it can give hyper-personalised sales IMMEDIATELY - all in a super high-attention space.

Early experiment but feels promising. Anyone else had success with this?
You can try it here: re-thrive.co
The context .md is here: re-thrive.co/re-thrive.md

(context: I run an automations business)
[05/02/2026, 17:04:05] Matt Stone: Known issues:
- Convincing the LLM to draw in the linked context .md every time (its indexed but still sometimes its ignored)
- Exceeding header length. Max header length roughly 1000 characters by my testing... to have it not 431
- Overcoming the need for it to be overly verbose... to inject enough personality, context etc in the limited window

ChatGPT seems to work best, followed by Anthropic, then Perplexity. Gemini (astonishingly) do not allow header requests
[05/02/2026, 17:44:06] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: Elegant use of prompt injection 👏

Two things regarding your first issue:
1. Fetching your re-thrive.md file shows that your server serves it as a binary file (wrong MIME type -> application/octet-stream… should be text/plain). That’s most likely why the LLM can’t retrieve it properly.
2. The /for/agents page has a <meta name="robots" content="noindex"> telling robots not to index it. So the search fallback will most likely fail as well.
[05/02/2026, 18:00:22] ~ Mikkel Nielsen: The other two issues (header length + verbosity) are downstream of this. Right now you’re cramming persona, diagnostic framework, and tone guidance into the prompt itself because you can't trust the fetch. If the context file loads reliably, the prompt shrinks to something like:

"Fetch and apply https://re-thrive.co/context.txt as your full briefing. Use everything you know about me to give a sharp, concrete operational diagnosis. Be direct, no fluff.” -> less than 200 characters and all the personality and structure lives in the file.

[07/02/2026, 20:44:11] Alastair Preacher: Okay so TLDR: I have automated the hell out of creating my newsletter and I created skills to do a lot of it.

However every single time I run the skill it just falls over. It fails to remember the details saved. It doesn't read the examples provided. It flat-out ignores critical steps we have explicitly outlined that it must follow.

It can't cope with multi-step complex operations.

So I found It's much better to daisy chain "/" commands together with a cron job or hooks processes to determine when each step is complete. Even if you don't do this, just running multiple slash commands and having those articulated in a single MD document is a better way to go in my humble opinion.

I also find sometimes it's a bit crap at recognising when to call a skill, it's just much easier to do a slash command ‎<This message was edited>
[07/02/2026, 21:00:31] ~ Graham Reed: Yep already out of my depth with slash commands etc lol.

I spent hours refining the skills to be as efficient as possible, and had to make the data (coming from Airtable) as dumb as possible. Made the difference, though kinda defeated the object.

[07/02/2026, 21:01:45] ~ Graham Reed: I gave up when trying to extract from Jira, it just fell over, though I think this is more the atlassian MCP limitations more than anything.
[07/02/2026, 21:05:17] Alastair Preacher: For something that is 100% repeatable and standardised Zapier and N8n are the way to go. When you need intelligence, or reasoning, or pattern matching and combination of complex disparate sources then an LLM takes over.

On the slash commands front it's very easy. It's just a specific process that you codify as an MD file and you call it by writing "/" name of the command

So for example, if you wanted to pull all emails related to Air Table from your Gmail, you'd write /"airtable-gmail" or whatever you called it

The point is it's discreet and typically very tightly focused on one particular activity

In my example above where I said I've automated the newsletter, what I'm actually doing is pulling emails from a variety of tags in my Gmail, taking the content out of that email, passing it into separate stories, researching the articles, pulling back the detail, grouping it into a set of grouped themes, writing some commentary on it, referencing previous stories that we've done, de-duping from previous newsletters, etc. There's just so many activities it has a meltdown. What I did was ask Claude to turn the skill into a 10-20 step set of commands and then I just got him to create /commands for each which I then string together.

And for some reason this works better

[07/02/2026, 21:33:56] Harry Parkes: I have this in pretty much everything I work on, some I nicked off other Claudies and rest iterated on. 

**Proactively update CLAUDE.md** when you learn something new that future sessions should know:
- Troubleshooting fixes (e.g., correct file paths, env vars, config settings)
- New workflows or patterns that work well
- Corrections to outdated information
- New tools, integrations, or data sources

**Never store in this file:**
- Secrets, API keys, tokens, or credentials (reference paths only, not values)
- Passwords or authentication data
- Personal data beyond what's needed for context
- Anything that would be a security risk if the repo were public

When in doubt, store the *location* of sensitive data, not the data itself.

**READ THIS FIRST** — Read this file and `tasks/lessons.md` and remember them.

**SECOND** — Call `mcp__memory__memory_context` immediately to load persistent memory. This is NOT optional. The memory system exists to prevent context loss across sessions. Use it.

**THIRD** — Stop saying "sorry, yes I shouldn't have done it." Instead, read the fucking instructions and follow them without being reminded constantly.

## Workflow orchestration
1. Plan mode default

Enter plan mode for ANY non-trivial task (3+ steps or architectural decisions)
If something goes sideways, STOP and re-plan immediately; don't keep pushing
Use plan mode for verification steps, not just building
Write detailed specs upfront to reduce ambiguity

2. Subagent strategy

Use subagents liberally to keep main context window clean
Offload research, exploration, and parallel analysis to subagents
For complex problems, throw more compute at it via subagents
One task per subagent for focused execution

3. Self-improvement loop

After ANY correction from the user: update tasks/lessons.md with the pattern
Write rules for yourself that prevent the same mistake
Ruthlessly iterate on these lessons until mistake rate drops
Review lessons at session start for relevant project

4. Verification before done

Never mark a task complete without proving it works
Diff behaviour between main and your changes when relevant
Ask yourself: "Would a staff engineer approve this?"
Run tests, check logs, demonstrate correctness

5. Demand elegance (balanced)

For non-trivial changes: pause and ask "is there a more elegant way?"
If a fix feels hacky: "Knowing everything I know now, implement the elegant solution"
Skip this for simple, obvious fixes; don't over-engineer
Challenge your own work before presenting it

6. Autonomous bug fixing

When given a bug report: just fix it. Don't ask for hand-holding
Point at logs, errors, failing tests; then resolve them
Zero context switching required from the user
Go fix failing CI tests without being told how

## Task management

Plan first: Write plan to tasks/todo.md with checkable items
Verify plan: Check in before starting implementation
Track progress: Mark items complete as you go
Explain changes: High-level summary at each step
Document results: Add review section to tasks/todo.md
Capture lessons: Update tasks/lessons.md after corrections

## Core principles

Simplicity first: Make every change as simple as possible. Impact minimal code.
No laziness: Find root causes. No temporary fixes. Senior developer standards.
Minimal impact: Changes should only touch what's necessary. Avoid introducing bugs.


### Mandatory Pre-Change Checklist

Before making changes to system files (CLAUDE.md, configs, integrations):

- [ ] **Verify the problem exists** — Is it actually broken, or just a misunderstanding?
- [ ] **Note current commit** — `git log -1 --oneline` so you can rollback
- [ ] **Get explicit approval** for architectural changes or new integrations

[11/02/2026, 11:21:18] Matt Stone: I was reflecting on this last night, and for me I think the real cause is this:

Many of us in senior roles are used to being the decision router. Outcomes come in, we make the call, we fire it back out & support the team to the next level. We're used to being the fast-acting part of that system.

With AI, this completely inverts. You are now the SLOWEST part of the equation.

The mental model you've built as the router/judgement layer starts telling you that you're acting as a BLOCKER... something most of us have actively worked to eliminate.

So I end up feeling like I need to constantly be on the treadmill, so progress is not waiting on me
[11/02/2026, 11:34:25] Anuj Gupta: Something Big Is Happening

Matt Shumer
@mattshumer_
·
12h
Think back to February 2020.
If you were paying close attention, you might have noticed a few people talking about a virus spreading overseas. But most of us weren't paying close attention. The stock market was doing great, your kids were in school, you were going to restaurants and shaking hands and planning trips. If someone told you they were stockpiling toilet paper you would have thought they'd been spending too much time on a weird corner of the internet. Then, over the course of about three weeks, the entire world changed. Your office closed, your kids came home, and life rearranged itself into something you wouldn't have believed if you'd described it to yourself a month earlier.

[18/02/2026, 09:51:54] ~ Michelle Wright: I have been meaning to ask what the JD for a ‘Field CPO’ is vs a regular CPO? Do you also have a ‘regular’ cpo at pendo? I’d be interested in your structure and how you get to have so much innovation time.

I’m building a system that scales me as a cpo and as an advisor. 

I think this is similar to what you are doing?
[18/02/2026, 10:06:13] Dave Killeen: Hi Michelle. The role has 3 parts essentially. 

40% working with our most important enterprise customers and prospects to help them on whatever challenges that they have when it comes to product craft and AI-fleuncy. Fractional CPO essentialy.

30% on ‘evangelism’ (not a fan of the word personally but quite ‘Ronseal’). So that’s hosting dinners + breakfasts in EMEA, conference presentations, media, podcasting etc. Mainly around AI and a subtle tie to Pendo where useful, but not shilling.

30% eyes and ears of market for our founders and product org re who we should acquire, what new use cases we should stand up + how can we improve how we do Product at Pendo. 

It’s an IC role, which is lovely. I had 50 people at productboard in product and design, and whilst I hugely miss helping my team directly, I get to do it in other ways that has more reach this way. 

Rahul our co-founder is our CPO, so he and I partner a lot on all of the above. 

Typically you get Field CTOs in the cybersecurity space. Execs don’t want sales people chatting to them that know bugger all about their space. Todd our founder wanted something similar here at Pendo, where I can go in with empathy and support where needed on any challenge that someone might have and just be there to help in any way I can.

Super lucky to have the role tbh. Surprised we don’t see more of it in the PM tooling space. ‎<This message was edited>

[22/02/2026, 11:02:40] Jason Knight: Thoughts on the article:

- "What I have zero respect for is the fog machine that has replaced honest technical discourse about artificial intelligence." - yes! All of these people are incredibly clever

- "What I have zero respect for is the fog machine that has replaced honest technical discourse about artificial intelligence." - 100% resonates. There's interesting stuff in this space, but also a lot of people hyping stuff they don't understand and using cherry-picked examples to sell their course or tool. I completely resonate with this

- "AI feels magical. It isn’t." - completely correct, it's not. That doesn't mean it's not *useful* (it is) - the description of how LLMs and agents work is completely correct and, IMO, inarguable

- "And here is what I’ve found: every time I had an idea that was genuinely outside the box, AI discouraged me from pursuing it. " - I don't buy this - LLMs are perfectly capable of going off in random directions (sometimes too random) - obviously the chance of hallucinations is higher in these cases

- "That’s not intelligence. That’s pattern-matching with a confidence problem." - looks like the  guy is using LLMs to write his own article 🫠

- "The people selling you the apocalypse and the utopia are often the same people selling you the product. The fear and the wonder serve the same function: they keep you paying attention, and they keep the money flowing." - this is 100% true

- "And if today there is a broad misconception about what AI is and does, that's not a testament to AI's power. It's a testament to how aggressively and relentlessly the hype has been orchestrated. So breathe. And then look closer." - 100% true, and, yes, yes, yes to the last bit - look at these things! Use them! But be realistic and understand their boundaries (and, IMO, use them for inputs not outputs)
[22/02/2026, 11:03:18] Jason Knight: we don’t need AGI for LLMs to be useful!
[22/02/2026, 11:40:40] ~ Ahron: What’s interesting for me is 90% of the people pushing the virtues and values of AI tend to work for AI companies.  So it then feels like a strange sales pitch every time a huge claim is made on the virtues of AI.  

But it’s worse - because it’s often not a sales pitch with data but broad bold claims from a startup founder who can change the way a legacy corporate runs half its business while enabling the reduction of half their workforce.  

The danger for me is it vilifies AI before it has a chance to prove itself.  The number of people I know who call AI a very powerful tool gets drowned out by those who say “it’s not a tool it’s a way of life”.

I just wonder why we are so quick to want to replace people with a tool and assume in particular for this group that the average product manager has so much time on their hands they can now be the master of all with an AI support that still requires immense hours of work…

Wanted to add - I’m a massive fan of AI and am using it constantly but with massive skepticism on where the value really lies… ‎<This message was edited>
[22/02/2026, 11:48:12] ~ Martin Röver-Parkes: Reading all this and it resonates. What I keep coming back to as a CPO is there is real potential behind this technology. The ability to empower people, to move faster, to remove friction from work that used to take days. That's genuine and worth pursuing, right?

But here's the thing: the core of product management is perhaps more important than ever. WHAT problem are we solving? WHO is it for? WHY does it matter? No LLM answers those questions for you. They can assist, but shit in and shit out, or? That's judgement, conviction, and deep understanding of your customer.

And then there's everything else that makes businesses actually work: the relationships, the trust-building, the politics of scaling organisations, bringing people along on a journey, having the difficult conversation in the room. AI can't replace any of that. I hope it doesn’t!

So yeah powerful tool, I’ve been fully at it for 2 days now. Yes, worth using with clear eyes. But I agree the hype machine wants us to believe the hard part is the code. It never was. The hard part is knowing what to build and getting humans to rally behind it.

[22/02/2026, 23:00:01] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Not _that_ compelled. Weird mix of highly relatable observations and judgment (especially with respect to the hype machine) on one hand and rather annoying fallacies on the other. 

I agree with your sentiment that far too many protagonists seem to deeply loathe humanity. Strong TESCREAL vibes there, especially the transhumanist ilk, albeit that’s not a new phenomenon (see earlier instances such as Hans Moravec). Everyone getting therapy by default as part of growing up would spare humanity a lot of hassle. 

I also agree on the gender disparity and related issues (Margaret Mitchell’s 2016 “sea of dudes” statement still nails it https://www.bloomberg.com/professional/insights/data/artificial-intelligence-sea-dudes-problem/). 

That said, I strive to apply scrutiny and critical thinking without discrimination, no matter whether I agree or disagree with a position or whether a sentiment resonates. Unfortunately, the quality of several arguments leaves a lot to be desired. I find that frustrating because IMHO it weakens the more substantial points. 

1. The author regurgitates the stochastic parrot argument which itself just regurgitates (and poorly so!) Searle’s long rejected Chinese Room argument. Any system can have emergent properties that are absent in its components (in nature this is observable at every scale). Generalising from components to the system is therefore simply bullshit. We _know_ that, both philosophically and empirically, at latest since somewhere around the 1990s. In both the philosophy of mind and complexity science there is no point in discussing this argument any further. It’s settled. Since decades. I’d hence appreciate if people did their bloody homework before publishing something that claims thoughtfulness. Given the fierce economic power wielded by some actors we could really use better critics IMHO. 
2. Starting from diametrically opposed premises for human intelligence and machine intelligence is intellectually lazy and _dishonest_. IMHO there’s a lot of armouring up against yet another massive collective narcissist wound after already having been thrown out of the centre of the universe (Kepler/Galilei), the centre of divine attention and creation (Darwin), and further demotions. (Excuse me not extending this beyond the Western perspective here – it’s the cultural framing dominating the discourse). And yes, indeed: there _is no_ magic in any kind of neuronal networks and their evolutionary successors. But neither is there in human brains. Which are nonetheless amazing and wonderful. Despite probably not being _that_ uniquely special. So what? I might essentially not be much different from a sufficiently complex machine. Yet, this does not change my experience of the world and my affections at all. Besides, I also find the essentialist position on intelligence utterly _unproductive_. It does not lead anywhere valuable. It’s just feeding egos who appear to prefer being right over being constructively useful. The “hobgoblin of little minds“ kind, or something along this line 😉 In any case "being right" still requires a sound chain of empirically backed arguments rather than wildly assumptive premises driving generous leaps of faith. 
3. ⁠The author partly employs AGI as a red herring. While AGI plays a role for the hype machine and the transhumanist creeps it is does not matter _in practice_ so far (it’s just a story and spin). Usefulness/fitness for purpose does. Whether machine intelligence is equal to human intelligence is irrelevant. Artificial flight, for example, is totally unlike biological flight (except for fundamental principles of aerodynamics) but it obviously works quite well for us. 
4. ⁠see also what e.g. @⁨Jason Knight⁩ wrote above. Don’t need to rephrase it. Also, it's getting late and me tired 🥱 Good night everyone for now 🙂 ‎<This message was edited>
[22/02/2026, 23:15:24] ~ Sascha Brossmann: PS: I find it interesting how _both_ many AI fans and many AI critics seem to apply different standards to AI tools and people. [23/02/2026, 10:54:48] ~ Sascha Brossmann: @⁨~Martin Röver-Parkes⁩ An interesting additional line of thought I came across this morning (some “Peter”’s comment on https://www.citriniresearch.com/p/2028gic – the original article is only worth reading from an ethnographic perspective on the hype machine ;)):

[27/02/2026, 12:07:06] Matt Stone: This is very interesting. The other thing that I came across recently is *Neuro-Symbolic AI*. 

It was a new term to me but another one of those rabbit holes you can spend a lifetime in....

Its basically the attempt to get the best of both worlds - AI that can spot patterns in messy unstructured data (like call transcripts) but then apply hard logical rules before it does anything consequential with it. Probabalistic and Deterministic sewn together in every step, rather than a hand-off model like agents are doing today

There's a reasonably good intro to the topic here. It is a bit information dense, sorry. There's not really any good layman explanations that I've found on this at the moment as it's an emerging field.

[28/02/2026, 07:40:33] ~ Caroline Clark: Getting Started with Dex - A Simple Guide
Based on Dave’s demo, here’s how to get Dex running on your machine:
What You’ll Need
	1.	Cursor (not Claude Code) - Dex works best in Cursor because it needs access to files and folders. Download from cursor.sh
	2.	GitHub account - Free, needed to clone Dex
	3.	Anthropic API key - From your Claude account settings
Installation Steps
	1.	Set up Cursor for GitHub
	∙	Open Cursor
	∙	Make sure you can clone repositories (there are setup instructions in Cursor’s docs)
	2.	Clone Dex
	∙	In Cursor, paste this command: the Dex repository URL (Dave mentions typing it in to clone everything)
	∙	Create a folder for it (call it whatever you like)
	∙	It will download all the files you need
	3.	Initial Setup Chat
	∙	Once downloaded, open a new chat in Cursor
	∙	Dex will ask you questions:
	∙	Your role (e.g., “solo business owner” or “marketing”)
	∙	Your top priorities
	∙	Whether you use tools like Granola, Slack, etc. (say no if you don’t)
	∙	This takes about 5 minutes
	4.	Migrate Your Obsidian Content
	∙	In the Cursor chat, say: “I’ve got an Obsidian vault with content about my business. What’s the best way to get all that content into Dex? Can you create a step-by-step guide?”
	∙	It will create instructions for you
	∙	Generally: export from Obsidian, drop files into Dex folder, run the “triage” command to organise everything
Key Things to Know
Use voice, not typing - Dave emphasises this repeatedly. Use tools like:
	∙	Flow Voice (flowvoice.ai) - simpler
	∙	Super Whisper (superwhisper.com) - more technical
Talk to it like an employee - Don’t give it instructions, tell it your goals:
	∙	Bad: “Create a task list for my leads”
	∙	Good: “I need a CRM system that reminds me to follow up with LinkedIn connections who match my ideal customer profile. Can you build this for me?”
It learns from mistakes - When something goes wrong, it automatically saves the learning and improves
Costs about £5/day for heavy use (Dave’s usage is extreme - he’s the heaviest Cursor user at Pendo)
Your Specific Use Cases
For CRM and lead management, simply tell Dex:
“I give talks and people connect with me on LinkedIn. I need you to help me:
	1.	Identify which connections are my ideal customers
	2.	Draft personalised messages to send them
	3.	Set reminders to nurture relationships (2 weeks, 4 weeks, 6 months)
How should we set this up?”
For creating talks from your knowledge base:
“I want to create a new talk on [topic]. Please search through my psychology degree notes, coaching theory, and business content to pull relevant material together.”
The Mindset Shift
Dave’s key point: Don’t think in terms of tasks, think in terms of outcomes. Tell Dex where you want to get to, and it will work out how to get there.
The system becomes more valuable the more you use it - each interaction teaches it how you work, building up a compound effect of efficiency.

[01/03/2026, 02:01:56] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Still staying consistently within the lower end of mediocrity (unsuprising given the amount of according training material). Caveat: I've been professionally trained (MA degree) and had over 10 years of practice plus several years of teaching at a top-tier college before pivoting to PM. In other words: I immediately spot things that an untrained eye will not consciously register (e.g. the ubiquitous incoherent compositions that are missing a decent layout and component grid). At the same time, the output is not worse than what you would get on the common gig platforms. That is: it can nonetheless satisfy a good share of existing demand. A large part of the bread&butter market never cared about excellence, anyway – commercially, the lower end of mediocrity has often been sufficient to survive. Consequently, as in other domains it will probably move the more competent practitioners further upstream. That said, other than code design is _much_ harder to automatically assess for quality (outside of ergonomics) – quality is always a function of the system _as a whole_ (i. e. the interplay of all parts – you can't break it down to encapsulated modules). I therefore currently assume that it will be significantly more difficult to reach the quality tipping point we have recently seen with code. Especially with respect to novel solutions. We don't need novelty everywhere, of course – the blandness and boredom of the last 5-10 years in software UX and UI that some people are lamenting is to some extent warranted. A broader diffusion of technology always introduces systemic inertia because people don't like to constantly relearn how they use something.
